Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number $\lambda$ which is known to both players. On the $n$th turn of the game (starting with $n=1$) the following happens:
\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_n \le \lambda n.
\]
    \item If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

We need to consider that both players have constraints, and they can choose numbers that affect future constraints. The game is a kind of "alternating constraints" game. The objective is to be the one who makes the opponent unable to move.

Let's formalize:

Let S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

The game proceeds for n=1,2,3,...:

- If n is odd, Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even, Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_n ≤ n.

If at some turn, the player cannot choose any x_n satisfying the constraint, the game ends and the other player wins.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (i.e., she can force a win no matter what Bazza does), and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy.

Note: The players know all previous choices. So they can adapt.

Observation: The constraints are cumulative. For Alice, the sum S_n must be ≤ λ n. For Bazza, the sum of squares Q_n must be ≤ n.

We need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game where each player can choose numbers to influence the state.

We can think of the game as a two-player game with state (S, Q) after each turn, but the constraints are on the total sum and sum of squares. However, note that the constraints are not symmetric; they alternate.

We need to find the critical λ where the outcome changes.

Let's try to analyze small λ.

We already saw that for λ > √2, Alice can win on turn 2 by choosing x_1 > √2 (since she can choose x_1 = √2+ε, and then Bazza cannot move because Q_1 = x_1^2 > 2, so Q_2 ≥ Q_1 >2, so he cannot choose any x_2). So for λ > √2, Alice wins.

For λ = √2, she cannot win on turn 2 because x_1 ≤ √2, so x_1^2 ≤ 2. If she chooses x_1 = √2, then Q_1 = 2, and on turn 2, Bazza can choose x_2 = 0, so Q_2 = 2 ≤ 2, and game continues. So she needs a longer strategy. We already found a strategy for λ = √2: choose x_1 = √2, x_3 = √2+ε, then after turn 3, Q_3 = 2 + (√2+ε)^2 = 4 + 2√2 ε + ε^2 > 4, so on turn 4, Bazza cannot move. So Alice wins. So λ = √2 is also winning.

Now consider λ < √2. Can Alice still win? She cannot win on turn 2. She might try to win on turn 4. She needs to make Q_3 > 4. She can choose x_1 = λ (maximum allowed), then on turn 3, she can choose x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 3λ, and Q_3 = λ^2 + x_3^2 > 4. Also, she must ensure that on turn 2, Bazza can move; he must choose x_2 such that Q_2 ≤ 2. He can choose x_2 = 0, which is safe as long as Q_1 = λ^2 ≤ 2, which is true for λ ≤ √2. So he will choose 0. Then on turn 3, Alice chooses x_3. The sum constraint: S_3 = λ + x_3 ≤ 3λ => x_3 ≤ 2λ. So she can choose x_3 = 2λ (max) to maximize squares. Then Q_3 = λ^2 + (2λ)^2 = 5λ^2. Need >4 => λ > 2/√5 ≈0.8944. So if λ > 2/√5, she can win on turn 4 by setting x_1 = λ, x_3 = 2λ. But note: For λ = 0.9, 5λ^2=4.05>4, so win. For λ = 0.8, 5λ^2=3.2<4, so not enough. This is a convex parabola, minimized at a=1.5λ, and maximized at endpoints a=0 or a=3λ. But a cannot be 3λ because after turn 1, S_1 = a ≤ λ, so a ≤ λ. Thus we need to analyze the game properly, considering that both players can choose numbers that affect the sum and sum of squares. The key is to find the critical λ where the outcome changes.

Let's denote the state after each turn. Since the constraints are cumulative, we can think of the game as a kind of "balancing" game. We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (she can force a win) and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy.

We already have some observations:

- For λ > √2, Alice wins on turn 2 by choosing x_1 > √2 (since she can choose x_1 = √2+ε, and then Bazza cannot move because Q_1 = x_1^2 > 2, so Q_2 ≥ Q_1 >2, so he cannot choose any x_2). So for λ > √2, Alice wins.

- For λ = √2, we found a strategy: choose x_1 = √2, then on turn 3, choose x_3 = √2+ε (with ε small enough such that S_3 ≤ 3√2). Then Q_3 = 2 + (√2+ε)^2 = 4 + 2√2 ε + ε^2 >4, so Bazza loses on turn 4. So Alice wins for λ = √2.

- For λ < √2, we need to see if Alice can still win. We found that for λ > 2/3, she can win on turn 4 by setting x_1=0, x_3=3λ. For λ = 0.6, 3λ=1.8, squares sum=3.24, not >4, so that strategy fails. But she can use a later turn: set x_1=0, x_3=0, x_5=5λ, then Q_5=25λ^2. For λ=0.6, 25*0.36=9>6, so win. So for λ=0.6, she wins. So it seems that for any λ>0, she can win by choosing k large enough such that λ^2(2k-1)^2 > 2k. That inequality is equivalent to λ > √(2k)/(2k-1). For a given λ, we can choose k such that √(2k)/(2k-1) < λ. Since the right side tends to 0 as k→∞, for any λ>0, there exists k such that this holds. So Alice can always win by this strategy. But wait: This strategy requires that on all previous odd turns, she sets x_n=0. That's fine. However, we must also consider that Bazza might choose positive numbers on his turns, which could increase S_n and reduce the room for her large number later. Then Alice's move: she chooses a = λ(2k+1) - S (since she wants to maximize Q). Then new state: S' = S + a = λ(2k+1), Q' = Q + a^2 = Q + (λ(2k+1) - S)^2. Then Bazza's move: he chooses b = √(2k+2 - Q') if Q' ≤ 2k+2. Then new state: S'' = S' + b = λ(2k+1) + b, Q'' = Q' + b^2 = 2k+2. So after Bazza's move, Q'' = 2k+2 exactly. Then the next state after even turn is (S'', Q'') with Q'' = 2k+2. So the process is: Starting with (S_0, Q_0) = (0,0). For k=1: Alice's move: a_1 = λ*1 - 0 = λ, so S_1 = λ, Q_1 = λ^2. Then Bazza's move: b_1 = √(2 - λ^2). Then S_2 = λ + √(2-λ^2), Q_2 = λ^2 + (2-λ^2) = 2. So after turn 2, Q_2=2. Then for k=2: Alice's move: a_2 = λ*3 - S_2 = 3λ - (λ + √(2-λ^2)) = 2λ - √(2-λ^2). Then S_3 = S_2 + a_2 = λ + √(2-λ^2) + 2λ - √(2-λ^2) = 3λ. So S_3 = 3λ. Q_3 = Q_2 + a_2^2 = 2 + (2λ - √(2-λ^2))^2. Then Bazza's move: b_2 = √(4 - Q_3) if Q_3 ≤4. To make M negative, he wants to make b_k as large as possible. The maximum b_k is √(2k - Q_{2k-2}). So if Q_{2k-2} is small, he can choose a large b_k. Alice wants to keep Q_{2k-2} large to reduce his b_k. So she will try to make Q large. She can do that by choosing a_k large. But she is limited by the sum constraint. So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.

So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.

To make M negative, he wants to make b_k as large as possible. The maximum b_k is √(2k - Q_{2k-2}). So if Q_{2k-2} is small, he can choose a large b_k. Alice wants to keep Q_{2k-2} large to reduce his b_k. So she will try to make Q large. She can do that by choosing a_k large. But she is limited by the sum constraint. So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.

So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.

If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n (i.e., no nonnegative real number satisfies the constraint given the previous choices), the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither wins.

We need to determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Interpretation: The game is a kind of "alternating constraints" game. At each step, the player chooses a number that must satisfy a cumulative constraint. The constraints are: For Alice (odd n), the cumulative sum S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i must be ≤ λ n. For Bazza (even n), the cumulative sum of squares Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2 must be ≤ n.

We need to consider that both players have constraints, and they can choose numbers that affect future constraints. The game is a kind of "alternating constraints" game. The objective is to be the one who makes the opponent unable to move.

Let's formalize:

Let S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

The game proceeds for n=1,2,3,...:

- If n is odd, Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even, Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_n ≤ n.

If at some turn, the player cannot choose any x_n satisfying the constraint, the game ends and the other player wins.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (i.e., she can force a win no matter what Bazza does), and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy.

Note: The players know all previous choices. So they can adapt.

Observation: The constraints are cumulative. For Alice, the sum S_n must be ≤ λ n. For Bazza, the sum of squares Q_n must be ≤ n.

We need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game where each player can choose numbers to influence the state.

We can think of the game as a two-player game with state (S, Q) after each turn, but the constraints are on the total sum and sum of squares. However, note that the constraints are not symmetric; they alternate.

We need to find the critical λ where the outcome changes.

Let's try to analyze small λ.

We already saw that for λ > √2, Alice can win on turn 2 by choosing x_1 > √2 (since she can choose x_1 = √2+ε, and then Bazza cannot move because Q_1 = x_1^2 > 2, so Q_2 ≥ Q_1 >2, so he cannot choose any x_2). So for λ > √2, Alice wins.

For λ = √2, she cannot win on turn 2 because x_1 ≤ √2, so x_1^2 ≤ 2. If she chooses x_1 = √2, then Q_1 = 2, and on turn 2, Bazza can choose x_2 = 0, so Q_2 = 2 ≤ 2, and game continues. So she needs a longer strategy. We already found a strategy for λ = √2: choose x_1 = √2, x_3 = √2+ε, then after turn 3, Q_3 = 2 + (√2+ε)^2 = 4 + 2√2 ε + ε^2 > 4, so on turn 4, Bazza cannot move. So Alice wins. So λ = √2 is also winning.

Now consider λ < √2. Can Alice still win? She cannot win on turn 2. She might try to win on turn 4. She needs to make Q_3 > 4. Also, she must ensure that on turn 2, Bazza can move; he must choose x_2 such that Q_2 ≤ 2. He can choose x_2 = 0, which is safe as long as Q_1 = λ^2 ≤ 2, which is true for λ ≤ √2. So he will choose 0. Then on turn 3, Alice chooses x_3. The sum constraint: S_3 = λ + x_3 ≤ 3λ => x_3 ≤ 2λ. So she can choose x_3 = 2λ (max) to maximize squares. Then Q_3 = λ^2 + (2λ)^2 = 5λ^2. Need >4 => λ > 2/√5 ≈0.8944. So if λ > 2/√5, she can win on turn 4 by setting x_1 = λ, x_3 = 2λ. But note: For λ = 0.9, 5λ^2=4.05>4, so win. For λ = 0.8, 5λ^2=3.2<4, so not enough. Let's denote the state after each turn. Since the constraints are cumulative, we can think of the game as a kind of "balancing" game. We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (she can force a win) and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy.

We already have some observations:

- For λ > √2, Alice wins on turn 2 by choosing x_1 > √2 (since she can choose x_1 = √2+ε, and then Bazza cannot move because Q_1 = x_1^2 > 2, so Q_2 ≥ Q_1 >2, so he cannot choose any x_2). So for λ > √2, Alice wins.

- For λ = √2, we found a strategy: choose x_1 = √2, then on turn 3, choose x_3 = √2+ε (with ε small enough such that S_3 ≤ 3√2). Then Q_3 = 2 + (√2+ε)^2 = 4 + 2√2 ε + ε^2 >4, so Bazza loses on turn 4. So Alice wins for λ = √2.

So it seems that for any λ>0, she can win by choosing k large enough such that λ^2(2k-1)^2 > 2k. That inequality is equivalent to λ > √(2k)/(2k-1). For a given λ, we can choose k such that √(2k)/(2k-1) < λ. Since the right side tends to 0 as k→∞, for any λ>0, there exists k such that this holds. So Alice can always win by this strategy. For example, suppose she tries to win on turn 2k by setting x_{2k-1} = λ(2k-1). Actually, the sum constraint after her move is S_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1). If S_{2k-2} is already large, then she must choose x_{2k-1} ≤ λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2}. So we need to consider that Bazza can try to force that at some odd turn, the sum before Alice's move exceeds λ n, so she cannot move. He can do that by choosing positive numbers on his turns, but he must respect his own square constraint. So the game is about who can force the other to be unable to move.

Thus we need to analyze the game as a kind of "alternating" game where each player can influence the state.

We can try to find the critical λ by considering the "worst-case" for Alice. She wants to make Q_n > n+1 at some even n. She can choose numbers on odd turns. But he can also choose numbers that increase Q_n, which might help her. So he will try to keep Q_n as small as possible while making S_n large enough to restrict her. So his optimal strategy is to choose x_n as large as possible subject to Q_n ≤ n, but also to maximize the sum S_n. However, he must respect Q_n ≤ n, so the maximum x_n he can choose given Q_{n-1} is √(n - Q_{n-1}). So he can choose x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) to maximize the sum increase. That is, he will set x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) on his turns. This is the maximum possible number he can choose without violating his constraint. This will increase S_n by that amount, and also increase Q_n by that square. So his strategy is to maximize the sum increase at each step.

Thus we can assume Bazza plays optimally: on each even turn, he chooses x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (if this is defined; if Q_{n-1} > n, he loses). This is the maximum he can choose, which is the best for him to make Alice's future moves harder. So we can model the game as: Alice chooses a_1, a_2, ... on odd turns; Bazza chooses b_1, b_2, ... on even turns, with b_k = √(2k - Q_{2k-2}) where Q_{2k-2} = sum of squares up to turn 2k-2. And the constraints: For odd n=2k-1, S_{2k-1} = ∑_{i=1}^{k-1} a_i + ∑_{i=1}^{k-1} b_i + a_k ≤ λ(2k-1). For even n=2k, Q_{2k} = ∑_{i=1}^{k} a_i^2 + ∑_{i=1}^{k} b_i^2 ≤ 2k.

Let's analyze the game with Bazza playing optimally (maximizing sum increase) and Alice playing a strategy of setting a_k = λ(2k-1) at some turn, but she must also ensure that on all previous odd turns, she sets a_i=0. Thus we need to analyze the interaction. This is a typical "alternating" game where the players can influence each other's state. We can try to find a "critical" λ by considering the "online" constraints and using a "strategy" that ensures that the sum of squares grows faster than the sum.

Perhaps we can think of the game as a kind of "pursuit" where Alice wants to make the sum of squares exceed the next even index, while Bazza wants to make the sum exceed the next odd index. This is similar to the "alternating sums" game where the players choose numbers to keep the cumulative sum below a line. There is a known result: In a game where players alternately choose nonnegative numbers, and the first player wants to keep the sum below a line, the second player wants to force the sum to exceed the line, the critical threshold is related to the "critical" slope. Let's try to compute the "safe" region for Bazza. He wants to survive indefinitely. So we need to see if there exists a strategy for Bazza such that for all k, D_{2k} ≥ -λ (so Alice can always move). Actually, for him to win, he needs to make D_{2k} < -λ at some point. So if he can achieve that, he wins. If he cannot, then the game may go on forever, which is a draw. So we need to find the threshold where Bazza's strategy succeeds.

We can try to find a "strategy" for Bazza that ensures that he can make the sum S_n exceed λ n at some odd turn. He can do that by choosing large numbers. But he is limited by his square constraint. So the maximum sum he can achieve after his k-th move is when he chooses b_i as large as possible, but they are limited by Q. So we need to find the maximum possible sum he can achieve given that Alice can also choose numbers. This is like a "online" game where the players choose numbers to maximize or minimize certain quantities.

Another approach: Since the game is infinite, we can consider the "strategy" for Bazza to try to make the sum S_n large. He can choose b_i as large as possible. The maximum he can choose is √(2i - Q_{2i-2}). So if he can make Q_{2i-2} small, he can choose a large b_i. Alice can try to make Q large to restrict him. So the critical λ is when Alice can force Q to grow fast enough that Bazza cannot make the sum too large. This is a typical "online" game.

Perhaps we can find the critical λ by solving a recurrence for the "safe" region. Consider the state after an even turn. Let's denote the "safety margin" M = λ(2k) - S_{2k}. He wants to make M negative. He can choose b_k = √(2k - Q_{2k-2}). Then M_{2k} = M_{2k-2} + 2λ - b_k. So the evolution of M depends on b_k. To make M negative, he wants to make b_k as large as possible. The maximum b_k is √(2k - Q_{2k-2}). So if Q_{2k-2} is small, he can choose a large b_k. Alice wants to keep Q_{2k-2} large to reduce his b_k. So she will try to make Q large. She can do that by choosing a_k large. But she is limited by the sum constraint. So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.

To make M negative, he wants to make b_k as large as possible. The maximum b_k is √(2k - Q_{2k-2}). So if Q_{2k-2} is small, he can choose a large b_k. Alice wants to keep Q_{2k-2} large to reduce his b_k. So she will try to make Q large. She can do that by choosing a_k large. But she is limited by the sum constraint. So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.

Alice wants to keep Q_{2k-2} large to reduce his b_k. So she will try to make Q large. She can do that by choosing a_k large. But she is limited by the sum constraint. So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.

To make M negative, he wants to make b_k as large as possible. The maximum b_k is √(2k - Q_{2k-2}). So if Q_{2k-2} is small, he can choose a large b_k. Alice wants to keep Q_{2k-2} large to reduce his b_k. So she will try to make Q large. She can do that by choosing a_k large. But she is limited by the sum constraint. So she will choose a_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k-2} if that is nonnegative; otherwise she chooses 0. So we need to analyze the game as a kind of "online" game.
